<figcaption><p>Panels A and B: Differential interface contrast (DIC) images at 400× magnification. Panels a, b: DIC images are 630× magnification. Purple circles indicate cell clumps of 4 or more cells.</p></figcaption></figure><figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="pone-0080677-g004"><h4 class="obj_head">Figure 4. Scanning electron microscopy analysis of <em>C. albicans</em> cultured in spaceflight and ground control conditions.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=3851762_pone.0080677.g004.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/7f95/3851762/2193df6e3e3b/pone.0080677.g004.jpg" loading="lazy" height="837" width="714" alt="Figure 4"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/pone-0080677-g004/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p>Cell clusters of spaceflight (A, B) and ground control (a, b) conditions are shown. Black arrow points to filament, white arrows indicate aberrant cell shapes, grey arrows indicate normal bipolar budding, and white dotted arrows indicate random budding scars. Magnification  = 5,000× for A and a, and 8,000× for B and b. C and c show images of spaceflight and ground control cells respectively at lower magnification (2,500×) to demonstrate the difference in space occupancy between the test conditions (3D architecture for spaceflight compared to flat structure for ground cultures).</p></figcaption></figure><p>
<em>C. albicans</em> ground samples exhibited a higher number of cells with a bipolar budding pattern (reflected by ongoing budding and budding scars), while more cells with multiple, randomly distributed budding scars were observed for spaceflight cultures (<a href="#pone-0080677-g004" class="usa-link">
<strong>Figure 4A</strong>
</a>
<strong>and a</strong>, white dotted arrows). Accordingly, genes involved in random budding of <em>C. albicans</em> were significantly affected by spaceflight culture. Since the multiple budding phenotype could indicate the generation of more daughter cells that are typically smaller, the cell surface area, width and length were determined for <em>C. albicans</em> cells grown in ground or spaceflight conditions, respectively. The average surface area for ground samples (6.6±3.0 µm<sup>2</sup>) was significantly higher than for flight samples (4.6±2.4 µm<sup>2</sup>) (1.4-fold, p&lt;10<sup>−9</sup>). In addition, <a href="#pone-0080677-g005" class="usa-link">
<strong>Figure 5A</strong>
</a> shows that a higher percentage of cells with a smaller surface area was observed for spaceflight cultures. For example, 80% of the spaceflight cells versus only 47% of ground cells had a surface area smaller than 5 µm<sup>2</sup> (<a href="#pone-0080677-g005" class="usa-link">
<strong>Figure 5A</strong>
</a>). To assess cell shape, we determined the width-to-length ratio. Ground control cells had a higher percentage of cells with a ratio above 0.8 (67.8% for ground versus 30.5% for spaceflight), indicating that more <em>C. albicans</em> cells grown in control conditions had a rounder morphology (<a href="#pone-0080677-g005" class="usa-link">
<strong>Figure 5B</strong>
</a>). It is important to note that ground control cells appeared more flat, compared to spaceflight cells, which showed a 3D organization (<a href="#pone-0080677-g004" class="usa-link">
<strong>Figure 4C</strong>
</a>
<strong>versus 4c</strong>). This could potentially explain, at least in part, a larger surface area for ground control cultures. Also, the increased presence of aberrant yeast forms was observed in spaceflight samples (<a href="#pone-0080677-g004" class="usa-link">
<strong>Figure 4A</strong>
</a>
<strong>and a</strong>, white arrow). The aberrant yeast forms in panels A and a are reminiscent of dying cells. However, post-flight viable cell counts indicated no differences between cultures exposed to microgravity and synchronous ground controls (i.e., 4.78×10<sup>7</sup> CFU/mL for flight samples and 5.94×10<sup>7</sup> CFU/mL for ground samples).</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="pone-0080677-g005"><h4 class="obj_head">Figure 5. Measurement of cell size and shape of <em>C. albicans</em> spaceflight and ground control cultures.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=3851762_pone.0080677.g005.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/7f95/3851762/45212e87531b/pone.0080677.g005.jpg" loading="lazy" height="840" width="720" alt="Figure 5"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/pone-0080677-g005/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p>(A) Surface area of spaceflight and ground cells, organized as percentage of cells per size range (1 µm increments). The percentages for ground and flight cultured <em>C. albicans</em> with a surface area between 0 and 5 µm are indicated. (B) Width-to-length ratio of spaceflight and ground cells, organized as percentage of cells per width-to-length range (0.1 increments). Results were obtained based on surface area and width-to-length determination of 143 ground control cells and 197 spaceflight-cultured cells.</p></figcaption></figure><p>Flow cytometry analysis demonstrated a 2.8-fold increase (p&lt;0.025) in forward scatter signal for spaceflight-grown <em>C. albicans</em> (<a href="#pone-0080677-g006" class="usa-link">
<strong>Figure 6</strong>
</a>), which is reflective of the observed increases in cell aggregation in spaceflight samples.</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="pone-0080677-g006"><h4 class="obj_head">Figure 6. Flow cytometry analysis of <em>C. albicans</em> flight samples and ground controls.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=3851762_pone.0080677.g006.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/7f95/3851762/2ca7fa94d95c/pone.0080677.g006.jpg" loading="lazy" height="708" width="704" alt="Figure 6"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/pone-0080677-g006/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p>Panel A represents a dot plot of <em>C. albicans</em> yeast cells grown at 30°C (to set the threshold for non-flocculated organisms). Panels B and C illustrate dot plots of ground and flight samples respectively. The Y-axis represents side-scatter and the X-axis forward scatter (FSC). Events with FSC values below the established threshold were considered single or budding yeast, whereas events above the established threshold were considered cell clusters.</p></figcaption></figure></section><section id="s3c"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Virulence</h3>
<p>Due to limited sample availability, a focused study to determine the effect of spaceflight culture on <em>C. albicans</em> virulence was performed by infecting mice via the i.p. route with a single infection dose grown under spaceflight/ground control conditions and monitoring the time to death. This targeted study indicated no differences between the virulence of spaceflight and ground cultures, as reflected in comparable mouse survival in both test conditions (<strong><a href="#pone.0080677.s002" class="usa-link">Figure S2</a></strong>).</p></section></section><section id="s4"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Discussion</h2>
<p>The presence of the opportunistic fungus <em>C. albicans</em> in the normal flora of astronauts could present an infectious disease risk during long-term missions. Indeed, microorganisms have been shown to enhance their virulence and/or display virulence-related phenotypes in response to culture in the low fluid-shear environment of both microgravity and microgravity-analogue culture systems <a href="#pone.0080677-Crabb1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Crabb1">[10]</a>–<a href="#pone.0080677-Wilson2" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Wilson2">[12]</a>, <a href="#pone.0080677-Castro1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Castro1">[20]</a>–<a href="#pone.0080677-Wilson3" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Wilson3">[22]</a>, <a href="#pone.0080677-Nickerson3" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Nickerson3">[24]</a>, <a href="#pone.0080677-Altenburg1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Altenburg1">[34]</a>, <a href="#pone.0080677-Searles1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Searles1">[35]</a>, <a href="#pone.0080677-Crabb3" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Crabb3">[73]</a>–<a href="#pone.0080677-Kim1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Kim1">[79]</a>. Moreover, as <em>C. albicans</em> causes a variety of mucosal and deep tissue infections in immunosuppressed patients <a href="#pone.0080677-Lim1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Lim1">[9]</a>, the decreased immune response of astronauts in-flight could further contribute to an increased susceptibility to microbial infections <a href="#pone.0080677-Gueguinou1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Gueguinou1">[1]</a>.</p>
<p>In addition to the application of spaceflight microbiology studies for infectious disease risk assessment in the astronaut population, these studies also entail applications to advance human health on Earth. Complementing conventional infectious disease research with spaceflight studies can serve to bridge gaps in our current understanding of host-pathogen interactions, given the unique ways in which both the host and pathogen respond to this extreme environment <a href="#pone.0080677-Gueguinou1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Gueguinou1">[1]</a>, <a href="#pone.0080677-Nickerson1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Nickerson1">[2]</a>, <a href="#pone.0080677-Nickerson3" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Nickerson3">[24]</a>. The low fluid-shear forces to which microorganisms are exposed during liquid culture in spaceflight and spaceflight analogues is relevant to environmental conditions encountered during their normal terrestrial lifecycles, including in the gastrointestinal, respiratory, and urogenital tracts of the host <a href="#pone.0080677-Nickerson2" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Nickerson2">[3]</a>, <a href="#pone.0080677-Soll1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Soll1">[42]</a>–<a href="#pone.0080677-Nauman1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Nauman1">[45]</a>. Thus, studying the responses of microbial cells to the microgravity environment of spaceflight holds potential for the discovery of novel infectious disease mechanisms that cannot be observed using conventional culture conditions, where the force of gravity can mask key cellular responses.</p>
<p>This study demonstrated that spaceflight culturing induced a self-aggregative phenotype (resembling the flocculation phenotype of <em>S. cerevisiae</em>) in <em>C. albicans</em> and altered a plethora of genes involved in stress and drug resistance; which is important for the virulence of this organism. The high prevalence of differentially expressed genes involved in biofilm formation and filamentation of <em>C. albicans</em> in response to spaceflight culture suggests that the microscopically observed self-aggregative phenotype could be reflective of biofilms. Indeed, transcriptional regulation of biofilm formation and filamentation is intertwined in <em>C. albicans</em>, and an increased flocculation phenotype is believed to be the result of hyphae-specific gene expression <a href="#pone.0080677-Bauer1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Bauer1">[80]</a>. <em>C. albicans</em> biofilm formation is divided into four distinct phases: (i) surface adhesion and colonization by yeast-form, spherical cells, (ii) microcolony formation on the attached surface by yeast-form cells, (iii) growth of pseudohyphae and hyphae in concert with synthesis of extracellular matrix, and (iv) dispersal of yeast-form cells to initiate biofilm formation off-site <a href="#pone.0080677-Nobile1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Nobile1">[53]</a>, . Microcolony formation on abiotic surfaces (structurally similar to flocculation) is estimated to take place 3–4 hours after initial adhesion, while formation of pseudohyphae and hyphae occurs at later time points (12–30 hours) <a href="#pone.0080677-Chandra1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Chandra1">[54]</a>. We hypothesize that at the 25-hour time point of fixation in this study (for gene expression/microscopic analysis), <em>C. albicans</em> may have been in the process of transitioning to the hyphal biofilm stage, which was not yet translated at the phenotypic level. In support of this hypothesis is the previous finding that <em>C. albicans</em> grown in LSMMG conditions exerted increased biofilm formation and biofilm-associated filamentation after long-term culture in the RWV bioreactor (4–5 days) <a href="#pone.0080677-Searles1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Searles1">[35]</a>. In microgravity-analogue conditions, biofilm formation was observed on the gas-permeable siliconized rubber membranes of RWV bioreactors, while in spaceflight samples, self-aggregation of microbial cells was observed. Interestingly, flocculation of <em>S. cerevisiae</em> has also been reported in LSMMG conditions, but detailed analysis of gene expression was not performed <a href="#pone.0080677-PurevdorjGage1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-PurevdorjGage1">[32]</a>. Furthermore, <em>P. aeruginosa</em> and <em>S. aureus</em> grown in LSMMG also displayed self-aggregative biofilm phenotypes <a href="#pone.0080677-Castro1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Castro1">[20]</a>, <a href="#pone.0080677-Crabb3" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Crabb3">[73]</a>, and <em>S.</em> Typhimurium formed biofilms during spaceflight culture <a href="#pone.0080677-Wilson1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Wilson1">[11]</a>. For <em>C. albicans</em>, key regulators of filamentation that were differentially regulated by long term culture in LSMMG (i.e., repression of <em>YWP1</em>, induction of <em>HWP1</em> and <em>BCR1</em>) were not differentially expressed in shorter term spaceflight-grown <em>C. albicans</em>; although the gene encoding the cell surface glycoprotein Als1 showed significant induction in both spaceflight and spaceflight-analogue cultures. Als1 is functionally and structurally similar to the major flocculation protein in <em>S. cerevisiae</em>, Flo11, and is an effector of filamentation, and a mediator of adherence and flocculation <a href="#pone.0080677-Fu1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Fu1">[62]</a>. The transcriptional regulation of self-aggregation has extensively been studied in <em>S. cerevisiae</em> given the associated industrial applications of this phenotype. Three main pathways have been proposed to regulate flocculation (via Flo11) in <em>S. cerevisiae</em>: (i) Ras-cAMP, (ii) MAP kinase (MAPK)-dependent filamentous growth, and (iii) main glucose repression pathway <a href="#pone.0080677-Verstrepen1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Verstrepen1">[82]</a>. In this regard, genes involved in the three main flocculation regulatory pathways were also found differentially regulated in spaceflight-cultured <em>C. albicans</em>. Therefore, Als1 could be a key mediator in the observed spaceflight-induced self-aggregative phenotype of <em>C. albicans</em>.</p>
<p>We also examined the expression of genes involved in the production of biofilm extracellular matrix proteins. While the complete composition and transcriptional regulation of the extracellular matrix of <em>C. albicans</em> biofilms remains to be unveiled, studies have shown the presence of carbohydrates, proteins and nucleic acid components <a href="#pone.0080677-Baillie1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Baillie1">[83]</a>–<a href="#pone.0080677-Hawser1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Hawser1">[85]</a>. A recent study identified three glucan modifying genes that play a role in glucan incorporation in the biofilm matrix <a href="#pone.0080677-Taff1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Taff1">[60]</a>, one of which, glucanosyltransferase (Phr1), was significantly upregulated in spaceflight conditions.</p>
<p>Another morphological change that was observed for spaceflight cultures of <em>C. albicans</em> was the presence of an increased number of cells with random budding scars as compared to more cells with a bipolar budding pattern for synchronous ground controls. This phenotype was also observed for <em>S. cerevisiae</em> exposed to spaceflight culture conditions <a href="#pone.0080677-Walther1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Walther1">[28]</a>–<a href="#pone.0080677-VanMulders1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-VanMulders1">[30]</a>. Polarized cell division is essential for the development of eukaryotes and prokaryotes, and typically takes place at the distal cell poles (180° from the birth site), termed bipolar budding, or at the proximal cell poles (adjacent to the preceding site of cytokinesis), termed axial budding <a href="#pone.0080677-Gimeno1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Gimeno1">[86]</a>, <a href="#pone.0080677-Chant1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Chant1">[87]</a>. Bipolar budding is believed to maximize nutrient exposure of the growing yeast cells <a href="#pone.0080677-Gimeno1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Gimeno1">[86]</a>, while axial budding facilitates mating and diploid formation <a href="#pone.0080677-Nasmyth1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Nasmyth1">[88]</a>. Specific mutations and environmental conditions cause random budding which is associated with loss of cell polarity, as reflected in a round cell morphology and cell separation deficiency, associated with production of cell clumps <a href="#pone.0080677-Chant1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Chant1">[87]</a>, <a href="#pone.0080677-GutierrezEscribano1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-GutierrezEscribano1">[89]</a>. As described above, enhanced cell clumping was observed for spaceflight cultures of <em>C. albicans</em>. In agreement with the random budding phenotype of <em>C. albicans</em> in spaceflight cultures, multiple genes involved in random budding of yeast were significantly affected. Interestingly, the enhanced presence of multiple budding scars could indicate the generation of more daughter cells in spaceflight conditions, which is supported by the smaller cell size of spaceflight-cultured <em>C. albicans</em>, and at the transcriptional level, by the induction of the daughter-cell specific transcription factor <em>ACE2</em> and downregulation of the G1 cyclin <em>CLN3</em> in spaceflight-cultured <em>C. albicans</em> (see above) <a href="#pone.0080677-Kelly1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Kelly1">[68]</a>. In yeast, asymmetric cell division results in the generation of smaller daughter cells as compared to the mother cell <a href="#pone.0080677-Hartwell1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Hartwell1">[90]</a>. Since the regulation of the G1 cycle is, in part, dependent on cell size; daughter cells require additional growth before the Start transition in G1. This process is orchestrated by a cell size-sensing module, in which Cln3 is the main regulator <a href="#pone.0080677-DiTalia2" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-DiTalia2">[91]</a>. The daughter-cell specific transcription factor, Ace2, has a direct negative regulatory effect on the expression of <em>CLN3</em>, which plays a role in delaying the G1 phase in daughter cells <a href="#pone.0080677-DiTalia1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-DiTalia1">[69]</a>. The enhanced presence of daughter cells could also indicate differential growth rate of <em>C. albicans</em> in spaceflight conditions. While at the time point of analysis, no differences in viable cell counts were recorded, more detailed monitoring of growth profiles are needed to determine if <em>C. albicans</em> altered its generation time in flight. It was hypothesized by Walther <em>et al</em>. that the random budding pattern in spaceflight cultures of <em>S. cerevisiae</em> could be explained by microgravity-induced changes in the cytoskeleton, which has been reported for a variety of mammalian cells (reviewed in <a href="#pone.0080677-Pietsch1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Pietsch1">[70]</a>). Indeed, the actin cytoskeleton is essential for bud site selection, and mutants in actin organization exert a random budding phenotype <a href="#pone.0080677-Ni1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Ni1">[67]</a>. In accordance with Walther and colleagues, we found that <em>C. albicans</em> exposed to spaceflight culture conditions downregulated several key genes involved in the actin organization and polymerization.</p>
<p>Several mechanisms of drug resistance have been described for <em>C. albicans</em> yeast cells, including differential expression of drug targets, efflux pump-mediated drug transport, and utilization of compensatory and catabolic pathways <a href="#pone.0080677-Ramage2" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Ramage2">[64]</a>, <a href="#pone.0080677-Sanglard1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Sanglard1">[95]</a>. Biofilm formation confers additional resistance in <em>C. albicans</em> through increased cell density, production of extracellular matrix proteins, and the presence of persisters <a href="#pone.0080677-Ramage2" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Ramage2">[64]</a>, <a href="#pone.0080677-LaFleur1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-LaFleur1">[96]</a>. In this study, genes encoding ABC transporters and multidrug efflux proteins (major facilitator family) were induced in spaceflight-cultured <em>C. albicans</em> (such as <em>CDR1</em>, <em>CDR4, CDR12</em>), which are involved in resistance to different classes of antifungals including polyenes (e.g. amphotericin B) and azoles. Also, spaceflight cultures of <em>C. albicans</em> showed downregulation of genes encoding ergosterol (<em>ERG6</em>, <em>ERG25</em>), which is a major drug target for this organism. Ergosterol is uniquely present in the membranes of yeast and fungal cells, and polyenes specifically target ergosterol in the fungal membrane, which creates pores and results in cell death <a href="#pone.0080677-Sanglard1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Sanglard1">[95]</a>. Downregulation of ergosterol levels in the cell membrane of sessile or biofilm-forming <em>C. albicans</em> contributes to the resistance of this organism to both polyene and azole antifungal agents. Interestingly, enhanced resistance of LSMMG-cultured <em>C. albicans</em> to amphotericin B was previously observed, which increased with the time of incubation under these microgravity-analogue conditions <a href="#pone.0080677-Searles1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Searles1">[35]</a>. In addition, <em>S.</em> Typhimurium showed induction of outer membrane porins, ABC transporters, and other genes involved in antibiotic resistance in response to culture in spaceflight conditions <a href="#pone.0080677-Wilson1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Wilson1">[11]</a>. Whether the observed differences in gene expression translate to a phenotype of <em>C. albicans</em> that is more resistant to antifungal drug agents remains to be determined.</p>
<p>We observed that a significant number of genes differentially regulated in response to spaceflight culture were involved in the oxidative stress resistance of <em>C. albicans</em>. Cap1 presumably played a role in the oxidative stress-associated gene expression since it has been shown to be involved in the oxidative stress response of <em>C. albicans</em>
<a href="#pone.0080677-Znaidi1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Znaidi1">[63]</a>, and more than 30% of the Cap1 regulon was affected by spaceflight. It would seem unlikely that increased gene expression related to oxidative stress resistance is due to the presence of increased oxygen levels since previously reported gene expression profiles of bacterial FPA cultures exposed to spaceflight indicated responses to microaerophilic/anaerobic conditions, presumably due to low fluid-shear levels and/or limited mixing in microgravity <a href="#pone.0080677-Crabb1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Crabb1">[10]</a>, <a href="#pone.0080677-Wilson1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Wilson1">[11]</a>, <a href="#pone.0080677-Kim1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Kim1">[79]</a>. In correspondence with our data, the spaceflight-induced proteome of <em>S. cerevisiae</em> comprised multiple proteins involved in oxidative stress <a href="#pone.0080677-VanMulders1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-VanMulders1">[30]</a>. Moreover, a recent study demonstrated that growth of <em>S. cerevisiae</em> in spaceflight in hyperoxic conditions resulted in extracellular release of glutathione <a href="#pone.0080677-Bradamante1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Bradamante1">[29]</a>. The observed increase in glutathione release was suggested to have occurred through activation of ion channels in response to cytoskeletal rearrangements in microgravity culture conditions <a href="#pone.0080677-Bradamante1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Bradamante1">[29]</a>. Spaceflight has been shown to modulate oxidative functions in other eukaryotic cell types, animal models, and astronauts <a href="#pone.0080677-Bradamante1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Bradamante1">[29]</a>, <a href="#pone.0080677-Baqai1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Baqai1">[97]</a>–<a href="#pone.0080677-Kaur1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Kaur1">[102]</a>. Collectively, our data indicate a potentially increased resistance of spaceflight-cultured <em>C. albicans</em> to antimicrobial agents and environmental stressors as compared to ground controls, which would need to be confirmed at the phenotypic level during future studies.</p>
<p>Despite the induction of a virulence-related phenotype of <em>C. albicans</em> in spaceflight conditions, we did not observe significant differences in virulence, as determined using an i.p. mouse model of infection. This observation could potentially be explained by the route of infection, the use of only a single lethal dose of <em>C. albicans</em> for the inoculation, and the short-term exposure to spaceflight. Indeed, i.p. infection is not a standard infection method for <em>C. albicans</em>, and was chosen given the unique time constraints associated with the spaceflight experiment. Alternatively, it is possible that spaceflight culture does not impact the virulence of <em>C. albicans</em>. Additional studies are needed to conclusively determine if spaceflight alters <em>C. albicans</em> virulence.</p>
<p>Since the RNA-binding protein, Hfq, was previously identified as a major regulator of the microgravity and/or microgravity-analogue response of <em>S.</em> Typhimurium, <em>P. aeruginosa</em> and <em>S. aureus</em>
<a href="#pone.0080677-Crabb2" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Crabb2">[21]</a>, we investigated the influence of spaceflight on expression of the LSm family of RNA-binding proteins in <em>C. albicans</em>, which are evolutionarily conserved eukaryotic homologues of Hfq <a href="#pone.0080677-Wilusz1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Wilusz1">[103]</a>. The gene encoding the LSm2 protein was the only LSm family member that was significantly affected by spaceflight culture of <em>C. albicans</em> under the conditions of this study. LSm2 is part of (i) the cytoplasmic LSm1-7 complex, which is important for mRNA decapping and decay, and (ii) the nuclear LSm2-8 complex, which is important for pre-mRNA and pre-rRNA processing <a href="#pone.0080677-Khusial1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Khusial1">[104]</a>–<a href="#pone.0080677-Ingelfinger1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Ingelfinger1">[107]</a>. In response to stress, there is a rapid shift of LSm proteins from the nucleus to the cytoplasm where the LSm1-7 complex concentrates within granular foci called processing bodies (P-bodies) <a href="#pone.0080677-Khusial1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Khusial1">[104]</a>–<a href="#pone.0080677-Spiller1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="pone.0080677-Spiller1">[108]</a>. To our knowledge, the role of LSm2 in the transcriptional regulation, virulence and behavior of <em>C. albicans</em> is unknown. Whether LSm2 regulation is involved in the spaceflight response of <em>C. albicans</em>, supporting a conserved transcriptional regulation between prokaryotes and eukaryotes, needs to be assessed in follow-up studies.</p>
<p>In summary, this study is the first to demonstrate that spaceflight culture conditions globally alter the gene expression profile of a eukaryotic pathogen and could potentially induce a virulence-related phenotype, and represents an initial step towards the infectious disease risk assessment of <em>C. albicans</em> during spaceflight missions. The effect of longer-term microgravity cultivation on the biofilm formation, filamentation and virulence phenotype of <em>C. albicans</em>, together with investigation of the potential spaceflight-activated transcriptional regulator Cap1 identified in this study is of interest for future research. Moreover, this study further reinforces the role that physical forces in the human body, such as low fluid-shear, could play in the infection process; insights that hold promise to fundamentally advance our understanding of infectious disease on Earth.</p></section><section id="s5"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Supporting Information</h2>

<p class="font-secondary"><em>This section collects any data citations, data availability statements, or supplementary materials included in this article.</em></p>
<section id="_adsm93_" lang="en" class="supplementary-materials"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Supplementary Materials</h3>
<section class="sm xbox font-sm" id="db_ds_supplementary-material1_reqid_"><div class="caption p">
<span>Figure S1</span><p>
<strong>Schematic of fluid processing apparatus (FPA).</strong> FPAs were used to initiate growth of <em>C. albicans</em> in spaceflight and ground control conditions (<em>activation</em>) and to fix <em>C. albicans</em> following growth in spaceflight and ground control culture conditions (<em>termination</em>). Panel A: The pre-flight assembly of the FPA with <em>C. albicans</em> in stationary phase. Panel B: The post-flight FPA in which <em>C. albicans</em> has been grown for 25 hours in space and on the ground and then fixed. Black boxes represent rubber stoppers, and grey boxes represent gas exchange membranes.</p>
<p>(JPG)</p>
